       1                 :            : /*-------------------------------------------------------------------------
       2                 :            :  *
       3                 :            :  * selfuncs.c
       4                 :            :  *        Selectivity functions and index cost estimation functions for
       5                 :            :  *        standard operators and index access methods.
       6                 :            :  *
       7                 :            :  *        Selectivity routines are registered in the pg_operator catalog
       8                 :            :  *        in the "oprrest" and "oprjoin" attributes.
       9                 :            :  *
      10                 :            :  *        Index cost functions are registered in the pg_am catalog
      11                 :            :  *        in the "amcostestimate" attribute.
      12                 :            :  *
      13                 :            :  * Portions Copyright (c) 2006-2009, Greenplum inc
      14                 :            :  * Portions Copyright (c) 1996-2009, PostgreSQL Global Development Group
      15                 :            :  * Portions Copyright (c) 1994, Regents of the University of California
      16                 :            :  *
      17                 :            :  *
      18                 :            :  * IDENTIFICATION
      19                 :            :  *        $PostgreSQL: pgsql/src/backend/utils/adt/selfuncs.c,v 1.243.2.1 2008/07/07 20:25:06 tgl Exp $
      20                 :            :  *
      21                 :            :  *-------------------------------------------------------------------------
      22                 :            :  */
      23                 :            : 
      24                 :            : /*----------
      25                 :            :  * Operator selectivity estimation functions are called to estimate the
      26                 :            :  * selectivity of WHERE clauses whose top-level operator is their operator.
      27                 :            :  * We divide the problem into two cases:
      28                 :            :  *              Restriction clause estimation: the clause involves vars of just
      29                 :            :  *                      one relation.
      30                 :            :  *              Join clause estimation: the clause involves vars of multiple rels.
      31                 :            :  * Join selectivity estimation is far more difficult and usually less accurate
      32                 :            :  * than restriction estimation.
      33                 :            :  *
      34                 :            :  * When dealing with the inner scan of a nestloop join, we consider the
      35                 :            :  * join's joinclauses as restriction clauses for the inner relation, and
      36                 :            :  * treat vars of the outer relation as parameters (a/k/a constants of unknown
      37                 :            :  * values).  So, restriction estimators need to be able to accept an argument
      38                 :            :  * telling which relation is to be treated as the variable.
      39                 :            :  *
      40                 :            :  * The call convention for a restriction estimator (oprrest function) is
      41                 :            :  *
      42                 :            :  *              Selectivity oprrest (PlannerInfo *root,
      43                 :            :  *                                                       Oid operator,
      44                 :            :  *                                                       List *args,
      45                 :            :  *                                                       int varRelid);
      46                 :            :  *
      47                 :            :  * root: general information about the query (rtable and RelOptInfo lists
      48                 :            :  * are particularly important for the estimator).
      49                 :            :  * operator: OID of the specific operator in question.
      50                 :            :  * args: argument list from the operator clause.
      51                 :            :  * varRelid: if not zero, the relid (rtable index) of the relation to
      52                 :            :  * be treated as the variable relation.  May be zero if the args list
      53                 :            :  * is known to contain vars of only one relation.
      54                 :            :  *
      55                 :            :  * This is represented at the SQL level (in pg_proc) as
      56                 :            :  *
      57                 :            :  *              float8 oprrest (internal, oid, internal, int4);
      58                 :            :  *
      59                 :            :  * The call convention for a join estimator (oprjoin function) is similar
      60                 :            :  * except that varRelid is not needed, and instead the join type is
      61                 :            :  * supplied:
      62                 :            :  *
      63                 :            :  *              Selectivity oprjoin (PlannerInfo *root,
      64                 :            :  *                                                       Oid operator,
      65                 :            :  *                                                       List *args,
      66                 :            :  *                                                       JoinType jointype);
      67                 :            :  *
      68                 :            :  *              float8 oprjoin (internal, oid, internal, int2);
      69                 :            :  *
      70                 :            :  * (We deliberately make the SQL signature different to facilitate
      71                 :            :  * catching errors.)
      72                 :            :  *----------
      73                 :            :  */
      74                 :            :

     546                 :            :  * n_skip = 1 are reasonable values.

    2223                 :            : 
    2224                 :            : /*
    2225                 :            :  * mergejoinscansel                     - Scan selectivity of merge join.
    2226                 :            :  *
    2227                 :            :  * A merge join will stop as soon as it exhausts either input stream.
    2228                 :            :  * Therefore, if we can estimate the ranges of both input variables,
    2229                 :            :  * we can estimate how much of the input will actually be read.  This
    2230                 :            :  * can have a considerable impact on the cost when using indexscans.
    2231                 :            :  *
    2232                 :            :  * Also, we can estimate how much of each input has to be read before the
    2233                 :            :  * first join pair is found, which will affect the join's startup time.
    2234                 :            :  *
    2235                 :            :  * clause should be a clause already known to be mergejoinable.  opfamily,
    2236                 :            :  * strategy, and nulls_first specify the sort ordering being used.
    2237                 :            :  *
    2238                 :            :  * The outputs are:
    2239                 :            :  *              *leftstart is set to the fraction of the left-hand variable expected
    2240                 :            :  *               to be scanned before the first join pair is found (0 to 1).
    2241                 :            :  *              *leftend is set to the fraction of the left-hand variable expected
    2242                 :            :  *               to be scanned before the join terminates (0 to 1).

    2609                 :            : 
    2610                 :            : /*
    2611                 :            :  * estimate_num_groups          - Estimate number of groups in a grouped query
    2612                 :            :  *
    2613                 :            :  * Given a query having a GROUP BY clause, estimate how many groups there
    2614                 :            :  * will be --- ie, the number of distinct combinations of the GROUP BY
    2615                 :            :  * expressions.
    2616                 :            :  *
    2617                 :            :  * This routine is also used to estimate the number of rows emitted by
    2618                 :            :  * a DISTINCT filtering step; that is an isomorphic problem.  (Note:
    2619                 :            :  * actually, we only use it for DISTINCT when there's no grouping or
    2620                 :            :  * aggregation ahead of the DISTINCT.)
    2621                 :            :  *
    2622                 :            :  * Inputs:
    2623                 :            :  *      root - the query
    2624                 :            :  *      groupExprs - list of expressions being grouped by
    2625                 :            :  *      input_rows - number of rows estimated to arrive at the group/unique
    2626                 :            :  *              filter step
    2627                 :            :  *
    2628                 :            :  * Given the lack of any cross-correlation statistics in the system, it's
    2629                 :            :  * impossible to do anything really trustworthy with GROUP BY conditions
    2630                 :            :  * involving multiple Vars.  We should however avoid assuming the worst
    2631                 :            :  * case (all possible cross-product terms actually appear as groups) since
    2632                 :            :  * very often the grouped-by Vars are highly correlated.  Our current approach
    2633                 :            :  * is as follows:
    2634                 :            :  *      1.      Expressions yielding boolean are assumed to contribute two groups,
    2635                 :            :  *              independently of their content, and are ignored in the subsequent
    2636                 :            :  *              steps.  This is mainly because tests like "col IS NULL" break the
    2637                 :            :  *              heuristic used in step 2 especially badly.
    2638                 :            :  *      2.      Reduce the given expressions to a list of unique Vars used.  For
    2639                 :            :  *              example, GROUP BY a, a + b is treated the same as GROUP BY a, b.
    2640                 :            :  *              It is clearly correct not to count the same Var more than once.
    2641                 :            :  *              It is also reasonable to treat f(x) the same as x: f() cannot
    2642                 :            :  *              increase the number of distinct values (unless it is volatile,
    2643                 :            :  *              which we consider unlikely for grouping), but it probably won't
    2644                 :            :  *              reduce the number of distinct values much either.
    2645                 :            :  *              As a special case, if a GROUP BY expression can be matched to an
    2646                 :            :  *              expressional index for which we have statistics, then we treat the
    2647                 :            :  *              whole expression as though it were just a Var.
    2648                 :            :  *      3.      If the list contains Vars of different relations that are known equal
    2649                 :            :  *              due to equivalence classes, then drop all but one of the Vars from each
    2650                 :            :  *              known-equal set, keeping the one with smallest estimated # of values
    2651                 :            :  *              (since the extra values of the others can't appear in joined rows).
    2652                 :            :  *              Note the reason we only consider Vars of different relations is that
    2653                 :            :  *              if we considered ones of the same rel, we'd be double-counting the
    2654                 :            :  *              restriction selectivity of the equality in the next step.
    2655                 :            :  *      4.      For Vars within a single source rel, we multiply together the numbers
    2656                 :            :  *              of values, clamp to the number of rows in the rel (divided by 10 if

    2663                 :            :  *              by the restriction selectivity is effectively assuming that the

    2866                 :            : /*

    2868                 :            :  * divided by total tuples in relation) if the specified expression is used
    2869                 :            :  * as a hash key.
    2870                 :            :  *
    2871                 :            :  * XXX This is really pretty bogus since we're effectively assuming that the
    2872                 :            :  * distribution of hash keys will be the same after applying restriction
    2873                 :            :  * clauses as it was in the underlying relation.  However, we are not nearly
    2874                 :            :  * smart enough to figure out how the restrict clauses might change the
    2875                 :            :  * distribution, so this will have to do for now.
    2876                 :            :  *
    2877                 :            :  * We are passed the number of buckets the executor will use for the given
    2878                 :            :  * input relation.      If the data were perfectly distributed, with the same
    2879                 :            :  * number of tuples going into each available bucket, then the bucketsize
    2880                 :            :  * fraction would be 1/nbuckets.  But this happy state of affairs will occur
    2881                 :            :  * only if (a) there are at least nbuckets distinct data values, and (b)
    2882                 :            :  * we have a not-too-skewed data distribution.  Otherwise the buckets will
    2883                 :            :  * be nonuniformly occupied.  If the other relation in the join has a key
    2884                 :            :  * distribution similar to this one's, then the most-loaded buckets are
    2885                 :            :  * exactly those that will be probed most often.  Therefore, the "average"
    2886                 :            :  * bucket size for costing purposes should really be taken as something close
    2887                 :            :  * to the "worst case" bucket size.  We try to estimate this by adjusting the
    2888                 :            :  * fraction if there are too few distinct data values, and then scaling up
    2889                 :            :  * by the ratio of the most common value's frequency to the average frequency.
    2890                 :            :  *
    2891                 :            :  * If no statistics are available, use a default estimate of 0.1.  This will
    2892                 :            :  * discourage use of a hash rather strongly if the inner relation is large,
    2893                 :            :  * which is what we want.  We do not want to hash unless we know that the
    2894                 :            :  * inner rel is well-dispersed (or the alternatives seem much worse).

    3013                 :            : /*
    3014                 :            :  * convert_to_scalar
    3015                 :            :  *        Convert non-NULL values of the indicated types to the comparison
    3016                 :            :  *        scale needed by scalarineqsel().
    3017                 :            :  *        Returns "true" if successful.
    3018                 :            :  *
    3019                 :            :  * XXX this routine is a hack: ideally we should look up the conversion
    3020                 :            :  * subroutines in pg_type.
    3021                 :            :  *
    3022                 :            :  * All numeric datatypes are simply converted to their equivalent
    3023                 :            :  * "double" values.  (NUMERIC values that are outside the range of "double"
    3024                 :            :  * are clamped to +/- HUGE_VAL.)
    3025                 :            :  *
    3026                 :            :  * String datatypes are converted to have hi and lo bound be constants, with
    3027                 :            :  *    the scaledvalue equally either hi or lo, depending on the value of isgt
    3028                 :            :  *    (done so that the caller will include the entire bucket in the final
    3029                 :            :  *     computed selectivity, even after inverting for the isgt case)
    3030                 :            :  *
    3031                 :            :  * The bytea datatype is just enough different from strings that it has
    3032                 :            :  * to be treated separately.
    3033                 :            :  *
    3034                 :            :  * The several datatypes representing absolute times are all converted
    3035                 :            :  * to Timestamp, which is actually a double, and then we just use that
    3036                 :            :  * double value.  Note this will give correct results even for the "special"
    3037                 :            :  * values of Timestamp, since those are chosen to compare correctly;
    3038                 :            :  * see timestamp_cmp.
    3039                 :            :  *
    3040                 :            :  * The several datatypes representing relative times (intervals) are all
    3041                 :            :  * converted to measurements expressed in seconds.
    3042                 :            :  *
    3043                 :            :  * isgt can be used by datatypes which cannot interpolate and instead must
    3044                 :            :  *   return an appropriate default
    3045                 :            :  *
    3046                 :            :  */
    3047                 :            : static bool
    3048                 :            : convert_to_scalar(Datum value, Oid valuetypid, double *scaledvalue,
    3049                 :            :                                   Datum lobound, Datum hibound, Oid boundstypid,

    3054                 :            :          * Both the valuetypid and the boundstypid should exactly match the

    3056                 :            :          * error out if either is not recognized.
    3057                 :            :          *
    3058                 :            :          * XXX The histogram we are interpolating between points of could belong
    3059                 :            :          * to a column that's only binary-compatible with the declared type. In
    3060                 :            :          * essence we are assuming that the semantics of binary-compatible types
    3061                 :            :          * are enough alike that we can use a histogram generated with one type's
    3062                 :            :          * operators to estimate selectivity for the other's.  This is outright
    3063                 :            :          * wrong in some cases --- in particular signed versus unsigned
    3064                 :            :          * interpretation could trip us up.  But it's useful enough in the
    3065                 :            :          * majority of cases that we do it anyway.      Should think about more
    3066                 :            :          * rigorous ways to do it.
    3067                 :            :          */

    3446                 :            : void

    3462                 :            : 
    3463                 :            : /*
    3464                 :            :  * This method returns a pointer to the largest child relation for an inherited (incl partitioned)
    3465                 :            :  * relation. If there are multiple levels in the hierarchy, we delve down recursively till we
    3466                 :            :  * find the largest (as determined from the path structure).
    3467                 :            :  * Input: a partitioned table
    3468                 :            :  * Output: largest child partition. If there are no child partition because all of them have been eliminated, then
    3469                 :            :  *         returns NULL.
    3470                 :            :  */
    3471                 :            : static RelOptInfo* largest_child_relation(PlannerInfo *root, RelOptInfo *rel)
    3472                 :            : {
    3473                 :        127 :         AppendPath *append_path = NULL;
    3474                 :        127 :         ListCell *subpath_lc = NULL;
    3475                 :        127 :         RelOptInfo *largest_child_in_subpath = NULL;
    3476                 :        127 :         double max_rows = -1.0;
    3477                 :            : 
    3478 [ +  - ][ -  + ]:        127 :         Assert(IsA(rel->cheapest_total_path, AppendPath));
    3479                 :            : 
    3480                 :        127 :         append_path = (AppendPath *) rel->cheapest_total_path;
    3481                 :            : 
    3482         [ +  + ]:        445 :         foreach(subpath_lc, append_path->subpaths)
    3483                 :            :         {
    3484                 :        318 :                 RelOptInfo *candidate_child = NULL;
    3485                 :        318 :                 Path *subpath = lfirst(subpath_lc);
    3486                 :            : 
    3487         [ -  + ]:        318 :                 if (IsA(subpath, AppendPath))
    3488                 :            :                 {
    3489                 :          0 :                         candidate_child = largest_child_relation(root, subpath->parent);
    3490                 :          0 :                 }
    3491                 :            :                 else
    3492                 :            :                 {
    3493                 :        318 :                         candidate_child = subpath->parent;
    3494                 :            :                 }
    3495                 :            : 
    3496 [ +  - ][ +  + ]:        318 :                 if (candidate_child && candidate_child->rows > max_rows)
    3497                 :            :                 {
    3498                 :        127 :                         max_rows = candidate_child->rows;
    3499                 :        127 :                         largest_child_in_subpath = candidate_child;
    3500                 :        127 :                 }
    3501                 :        318 :         }
    3502                 :            : 
    3503                 :        127 :         return largest_child_in_subpath;
    3504                 :            : }
    3505                 :            : 
    3506                 :            : /*
    3507                 :            :  * The purpose of this method is to make the statistics (on a specific column) of a child partition
    3508                 :            :  * representative of the parent relation. This entails the following assumptions:
    3509                 :            :  * 1.  if ndistinct<=-1.0 in child partition, the column is a unique column in the child partition. We
    3510                 :            :  *         expect the column to remain distinct in the master as well.
    3511                 :            :  * 2.  if -1.0 < ndistinct < 0.0, the absolute number of ndistinct values in the child partition is a fraction
    3512                 :            :  *     of the number of rows in the partition. We expect that the absolute number of ndistinct in the master
    3513                 :            :  *     to stay the same. Therefore, we convert this to a positive number.
    3514                 :            :  *     The method get_variable_numdistinct will multiply this by the number of tuples in the master relation.
    3515                 :            :  * 3.  if ndistinct is positive, it indicates a small absolute number of distinct values. We expect these
    3516                 :            :  *         values to be repeated in all partitions. Therefore, we expect no change in the ndistinct in the master.
    3517                 :            :  *

    4068                 :            : /*-------------------------------------------------------------------------
    4069                 :            :  *
    4070                 :            :  * Pattern analysis functions
    4071                 :            :  *
    4072                 :            :  * These routines support analysis of LIKE and regular-expression patterns
    4073                 :            :  * by the planner/optimizer.  It's important that they agree with the
    4074                 :            :  * regular-expression code in backend/regex/ and the LIKE code in
    4075                 :            :  * backend/utils/adt/like.c.  Also, the computation of the fixed prefix
    4076                 :            :  * must be conservative: if we report a string longer than the true fixed
    4077                 :            :  * prefix, the query may produce actually wrong answers, rather than just
    4078                 :            :  * getting a bad selectivity estimate!

    4080                 :            :  * Note that the prefix-analysis functions are called from

    4292                 :            : /*
    4293                 :            :  * Estimate the selectivity of a fixed prefix for a pattern match.
    4294                 :            :  *
    4295                 :            :  * A fixed prefix "foo" is estimated as the selectivity of the expression
    4296                 :            :  * "variable >= 'foo' AND variable < 'fop'" (see also indxpath.c).
    4297                 :            :  *
    4298                 :            :  * The selectivity estimate is with respect to the portion of the column
    4299                 :            :  * population represented by the histogram --- the caller must fold this
    4300                 :            :  * together with info about MCVs and NULLs.
    4301                 :            :  *
    4302                 :            :  * We use the >= and < operators from the specified btree opfamily to do the
    4303                 :            :  * estimation.  The given variable and Const must be of the associated
    4304                 :            :  * datatype.
    4305                 :            :  *
    4306                 :            :  * XXX Note: we make use of the upper bound to estimate operator selectivity
    4307                 :            :  * even if the locale is such that we cannot rely on the upper-bound string.
    4308                 :            :  * The selectivity only needs to be approximately right anyway, so it seems
    4309                 :            :  * more useful to use the upper-bound code than not.
    4310                 :            :  */
    4311                 :            : static Selectivity
    4312                 :            : prefix_selectivity(VariableStatData *vardata,
    4313                 :            :                                    Oid vartype, Oid opfamily, Const *prefixcon)
    4314                 :            : {
    4315                 :            :         Selectivity prefixsel;

    4321                 :            :                                                                  BTGreaterEqualStrategyNumber);
    4322         [ -  + ]:        343 :         if (cmpopr == InvalidOid)
    4323                 :          0 :                 elog(ERROR, "no >= operator for opfamily %u", opfamily);
    4324                 :        343 :         fmgr_info(get_opcode(cmpopr), &opproc);
    4325                 :            : 
    4326                 :        343 :         prefixsel = ineq_histogram_selectivity(vardata, &opproc, true,
    4327                 :        343 :                                                                                    prefixcon->constvalue,
    4328                 :        343 :                                                                                    prefixcon->consttype);
    4329                 :            : 
    4330         [ +  + ]:        343 :         if (prefixsel <= 0.0)
    4331                 :            :         {
    4332                 :            :                 /* No histogram is present ... return a suitable default estimate */
    4333                 :         59 :                 return 0.005;

    4379                 :            : 
    4380                 :            : /*
    4381                 :            :  * Estimate the selectivity of a pattern of the specified type.
    4382                 :            :  * Note that any fixed prefix of the pattern will have been removed already,
    4383                 :            :  * so actually we may be looking at just a fragment of the pattern.
    4384                 :            :  *
    4385                 :            :  * For now, we use a very simplistic approach: fixed characters reduce the
    4386                 :            :  * selectivity a good deal, character ranges reduce it a little,
    4387                 :            :  * wildcards (such as % for LIKE or .* for regex) increase it.
    4388                 :            :  *
    4389                 :            :  * CDB: There is a gradual lessening of the change in selectivity as more
    4390                 :            :  * fixed characters or character ranges are added.
    4391                 :            :  */
    4392                 :            : 
    4393                 :            : #define FIXED_CHAR_SEL  0.20    /* about 1/5 */
    4394                 :            : #define CHAR_RANGE_SEL  0.25
    4395                 :            : #define ANY_CHAR_SEL    0.99    /* not 1, since it won't match end-of-string */
    4396                 :            : #define FULL_WILDCARD_SEL 5.0
    4397                 :            : #define CDB_RANCHOR_SEL 0.95
    4398                 :            : #define CDB_ROLLOFF_SEL 0.14
    4399                 :            :

    4568                 :            : 
    4569                 :            : /*
    4570                 :            :  * Try to generate a string greater than the given string or any
    4571                 :            :  * string it is a prefix of.  If successful, return a palloc'd string
    4572                 :            :  * in the form of a Const node; else return NULL.
    4573                 :            :  *
    4574                 :            :  * The caller must provide the appropriate "less than" comparison function
    4575                 :            :  * for testing the strings.
    4576                 :            :  *
    4577                 :            :  * The key requirement here is that given a prefix string, say "foo",
    4578                 :            :  * we must be able to generate another string "fop" that is greater than
    4579                 :            :  * all strings "foobar" starting with "foo".  We can test that we have
    4580                 :            :  * generated a string greater than the prefix string, but in non-C locales
    4581                 :            :  * that is not a bulletproof guarantee that an extension of the string might
    4582                 :            :  * not sort after it; an example is that "foo " is less than "foo!", but it
    4583                 :            :  * is not clear that a "dictionary" sort ordering will consider "foo!" less
    4584                 :            :  * than "foo bar".    CAUTION: Therefore, this function should be used only for
    4585                 :            :  * estimation purposes when working in a non-C locale.
    4586                 :            :  *
    4587                 :            :  * To try to catch most cases where an extended string might otherwise sort
    4588                 :            :  * before the result value, we determine which of the strings "Z", "z", "y",
    4589                 :            :  * and "9" is seen as largest by the locale, and append that to the given
    4590                 :            :  * prefix before trying to find a string that compares as larger.
    4591                 :            :  *
    4592                 :            :  * If we max out the righthand byte, truncate off the last character
    4593                 :            :  * and start incrementing the next.  For example, if "z" were the last
    4594                 :            :  * character in the sort order, then we could produce "foo" as a
    4595                 :            :  * string greater than "fonz".
    4596                 :            :  *
    4597                 :            :  * This could be rather slow in the worst case, but in most cases we
    4598                 :            :  * won't have to try more than one or two strings before succeeding.
    4599                 :            :  */
